Maturidi's Argument for Quraysh in Imama
Maturidi explains why the imam must be from Quraysh despite piety being the primary criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the imam must be from Quraysh despite piety being the primary criterion, and the reasons he provides from revelation, history, and social practice.

Core Idea
Piety is ideal, but prophecy and political necessity designate Quraysh for imama.

Maturidi begins by stating the ideal religious criterion for imama: the most pious and knowledgeable person should lead. However, he immediately points to the Prophet's explicit statement that the imams are from Quraysh. This lesson explores the reasons Maturidi offers to reconcile the ideal with the specific designation, showing how religious and political considerations combine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the flow from ideal to specific reasons.
Pay attention to the shift after 'لكن'.
2
Identify the ideal criteria
Find where Maturidi lists the qualities the imam should ideally have (الأتقى، الأورع، الأبصر بالأمور، الأعلم بالمصالح). Note the Quranic evidence cited.
3
Note the prophetic override
Observe how Maturidi introduces the Prophet's statement that the imams are from Quraysh and the Companions' practice. This sets up the need for reasons.
This is the key pivot.
4
Understand the reasons
Maturidi gives several reasons. List them as you read: political need, Quran language, historical precedent, and social/lineage factors.
The reasons are introduced with 'لأوجه: أحدها'.
5
Reflect on the argument
Consider how Maturidi balances the ideal with the specific. Why is this important for the doctrine of imama?
Think about the role of political authority in religious leadership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأي لأبي منصور الماتريدي: وشيخنا أبو منصور الماتريدي قال: كان ينبغي من طريق الدين أن ينظر إلى الأتقى والأورع والأبصر بالأمور والأعلم بالمصالح فتعقد له الإمامة من كان هو على شهادة كتاب الله تعالى: (إِنَّ أَكْرَمَكُمْ عِندَ اللَّهِ أَنْقَنَكُمْ)، وعلى من اتصف به أمانة الأبضاع والأموال، والأمانة يتصل أداؤها بالتقوى، فيجب أن يكون هو المنظور إليه، لكن النبي الله يذكر أن الأئمة من قريش وأصحاب رسول الله طلبوا من قريش وعقدوا لهم فصرف الأمر إليهم لأوجه: أحدها: أن الإمامة مع أمر الدين فيها أمر الملك والسياسة فاحتيج في ذلك التقوى إلى جنس لا يزهد فيه ولا يوقف عن أصله، فرد ذلك إلى الجنس الذي لم يزل حري فيهم منذ عرفوا الجلالة والقدر، مع ما قيل إن القرآن نزل بلغة قريش، ومما تبين أنه يجب فيه اعتبار الأمرين ما لم يزل عرفت النبوة في قوم والملك في قوم كان أمر السياسة في أيدي الملوك والديانة في أدي الأنبياء عليهم السلام على ما قالوا لنبي لهم: ابْعَثْ لَنَا مَلِكًا تُقَتِلْ فِي سَبِيلِ اللهِ الآية، فكذا في هذا كان أمر الديانة في كل من قام به، وكان أمر الملك والسياسة في قوم مخصوصين ظهرت جلالة أقدارهم في الخلق. يحققه: أن الجنس والنسب أحدهما يدعو إلى المكارم والمصالح، ويزجر عن القبائح والفواحش، فمن له الزواجر والأوامر من وجوه كأن يكون في متعالم الأمر أو في العهد وأصول الإمامة، ثم فضل العلماء قريشًا على غيرهم من القبائل والشعوب في باب التناكح، وعلى ذلك كان أمر البراز عندهم أنهم كانوا لم يكونوا أكفاء لهم، وعلى ذلك عاملهم رسول الله عليه السلام فعلى ذلك يرون غيرهم
An opinion of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: And our master Abu Mansur al-Maturidi said: It would be appropriate from the way of religion to look at the most pious, the most abstinent, the most insightful in affairs, and the most knowledgeable in interests, and to appoint as imam whoever possesses that, according to the testimony of Allah's Book: 'Indeed, the most noble of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous of you,' and according to whoever is characterized by trustworthiness in matters and wealth; and trustworthiness's fulfilment is connected to piety, so he must be the one looked to. However, the Prophet, peace be upon him, mentions that the imams are from Quraysh, and the Companions of the Messenger of Allah sought [the imam] from Quraysh and pledged to them, so they turned the matter over to them for several reasons. One of them: that imama, along with the matter of religion, includes the matter of kingship and politics, so there was a need, in addition to piety, for a [tribal] group that is not disdained and whose origin is not stopped [i.e., respected]. So the matter was referred to the group that has always been keen [on dignity] since they [Quraysh] were known for majesty and status, along with what has been said: the Quran was revealed in the language of Quraysh. And among what clarifies that both matters must be considered is that it has always been that prophethood was known among one people and kingship among another: the matter of politics was in the hands of kings and religion in the hands of prophets, peace be upon them, as they said to a prophet of theirs: 'Send for us a king that we may fight in the way of Allah' - the verse. Similarly, in this [imama], the matter of religion is in whoever carries it out, and the matter of kingship and politics is in a specific people whose majesty of status has appeared among creation. He confirms it: that lineage and nobility invite to noble deeds and interests and prevent from ugly deeds and immoralities. So whoever possesses deterrents and commands from aspects such as being in the well-known [position] of the matter or in the covenant and principles of imama... Then the scholars gave preference to Quraysh over other tribes and peoples in the matter of marriage, and accordingly the matter of dueling [or matching] among them was that they were not considered equals to others, and accordingly the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, treated them, and accordingly they consider others.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's argument for why the imam must be from Quraysh, despite the ideal criterion being piety. The passage begins by stating that from a religious perspective, one should consider the most pious, abstinent, insightful, and knowledgeable person for imama, citing the Quranic verse 'the most noble is the most righteous' and emphasizing trustworthiness linked to piety. However, the Prophet explicitly stated that the imams are from Quraysh. The Companions acted on this by seeking the imam from Quraysh and giving them the pledge. Maturidi then gives several reasons for this: First, because imama involves both religious and political/political matters, there is a need not only for piety but also for a tribal group that commands respect and dignity; Quraysh have always possessed this. Second, the Quran was revealed in their language. Third, historically, prophethood and kingship have been separate — prophets were responsible for religion, kings for politics — but in the imama of Quraysh, both aspects combine. Fourth, lineage and nobility encourage good deeds and restrain evil; thus, a person from a noble lineage will have internal deterrents. Then Maturidi notes that scholars gave preference to Quraysh in marriage, considering them not equal to other tribes, and the Prophet himself treated them accordingly. Therefore, Quraysh are uniquely suited for imama. The argument follows the logical order: ideal principle → prophetic designation → reasons (religious-political necessity, language, historical precedent, lineage, and social preference).

Key Terms

الأورع
الأبصر بالأمور
الأعلم بالمصالح
الجنس
النسب
الملك والسياسة
الديانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since piety is the primary criterion, any pious person regardless of tribe qualifies.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the Prophet's explicit designation of Quraysh overrides the general principle, and he provides pragmatic and scriptural reasons for it.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is one reason the imam must be from Quraysh despite piety being the ideal?
A) Only Quraysh possess true piety.
B) Imama involves both religion and politics, requiring a tribe with dignity and respect.
C) The Quran was revealed in Arabic, not specifically Quraysh language.
D) The Companions forced the matter to Quraysh despite the Prophet's wishes.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that while piety is the ideal criterion for imama, the Prophet's designation, combined with political and historical realities, necessitates that the imam be from Quraysh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose a leader in your own life (e.g., community head, manager), do you instinctively prioritize their piety or their ability to get things done? How does Maturidi’s balance of both criteria challenge your usual tendency?
?
Maturidi acknowledges that Quraysh were chosen partly for their social status and respect. Are you willing to accept a leader who is less pious but more effective in governance, or do you insist on the ideal? What does your answer reveal about your own values?
?
If you were to apply Maturidi’s reasoning to a modern leader, what ‘tribal’ or structural factors would you consider alongside personal piety? Are you neglecting any such factors in your own judgments of leaders today?

The First Reason for Restricting Imamate to Quraysh
The author presents the primary argument for Quraysh restriction, combining religious and political authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the primary argument that the imamate combines religion and kingship, requiring a lineage like Quraysh that possesses both spiritual and worldly authority.

Core Idea
The imamate unites religion and kingship – its leader must come from a tribe rooted in both.

Although the Quran declares piety as the true measure of nobility, the Prophet ﷺ specifically stated that the imams are from Quraysh. This lesson explores the first and most foundational justification: the imamate is not merely a religious office but also a political one, requiring a lineage with both religious reverence and worldly power. The author argues that Quraysh uniquely fit this description, supported by the Quran's revelation in their language and an analogy from the Israelites.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening contrast
Notice how the author begins with the Quranic verse on piety, then pivots to the Prophet's statement about Quraysh. This sets up the tension this lesson resolves.
The author is not contradicting the Quran but showing that imamate has additional requirements beyond piety.
2
Understand the first reason
The imamate includes both religion (deen) and kingship (mulk) and politics (siyasa). Thus, a leader must come from a tribe that is not overly ascetic but possesses both religious respect and worldly capability.
Quraysh had 'jallāla wa qadr' – majesty and ability – historically.
3
Follow the Israelite analogy
The author draws a parallel from the Israelites who asked for a king while prophetic authority was separate. In Islam, the imam combines both roles, so the tribe must be suitable for both.
This shows the principle of considering both religious and political aspects.
4
See the supporting evidence
The Quran was revealed in the Quraysh language, and scholars recognized their superiority in marriage compatibility, indicating their elevated status.
This reinforces that Quraysh are the proper lineage for leadership.

على شهادة كتاب الله تعالى: (إِنَّ أَكْرَمَكُمْ عِندَ اللَّهِ أَنْقَنَكُمْ )، وعلى من اتصف به أمانة الأبضاع والأموال، والأمانة يتصل أداؤها بالتقوى، فيجب أن يكون هو المنظور إليه، لكن النبي الله يذكر أن الأئمة من قريش وأصحاب رسول الله طلبوا من قريش وعقدوا لهم فصرف الأمر إليهم لأوجه: مع :أحدها: أن الإمامة مع أمر الدين فيها أمر الملك والسياسة فاحتيج في ذلك التقوى إلى جنس لا يزهد فيه ولا يوقف عن أصله، فرد ذلك إلى الجنس الذي لم يزل حري فيهم منذ عرفوا الجلالة والقدر، مع ما قيل إن القرآن نزل بلغة قريش، ومما تبين أنه يجب فيه اعتبار الأمرين ما لم يزل عرفت النبوة في قوم والملك في قوم كان أمر السياسة في أيدي الملوك والديانة في أدي الأنبياء عليهم السلام على ما قالوا لنبي لهم: ابْعَثْ لَنَا مَلِكًا تُقَتِلْ فِي سَبِيلِ اللهِ الآية، فكذا في هذا كان أمر الديانة في كل من قام به، وكان أمر الملك والسياسة في قوم مخصوصين ظهرت جلالة أقدارهم في الخلق. يحققه : أن الجنس والنسب أحدهما يدعو إلى المكارم والمصالح، ويزجر عن القبائح والفواحش، فمن له الزواجر والأوامر من وجوه كأن يكون في متعالم الأمر أو في العهد وأصول الإمامة، ثم فضل العلماء قريشًا على غيرهم من القبائل والشعوب في باب التناكح ، وعلى ذلك كان أمر البراز عندهم أنهم كانوا لم يكونوا أكفاء لهم ، وعلى ذلك عاملهم رسول الله عليه السلام فعلى ذلك يرون غيرهم حق أمر الخلافة، وقد يحتمل التخصيص - والله أعلم - وجهين آخرين: أحدهما: أن طلب ذلك في جميع القبائل والآفاق أمر عسير فخفف حيث رد إلى قبيلة واحدة ودفع عنهم به أعظم المؤن، ثم كان المعروف من أمر هذه القبيلة أنها ترجع إلى بقعة يسهل على الناس النظر في جميع من يسكنها والظفر بمن يصلح للأمر مما إذا رد إلى الجملة وجب نظر ذلك في جميع البقاع والقبائل، ولمن تكلف البلوغ إلى المقصود يوجب تضييع الأمور وما لأجله احتيج إلى الأئمة، فلذلك اختار لهم الطلب فيهم. والثاني: يعلمه قد علم أنه يوجد فيهم من يصلح لأمور المسلمين أبدًا فأشار إليهم بما علم أن المطلوب يوجد فيهم لو أنصفوا وأمعنوا الطلب.
Upon the testimony of the Book of Allah Almighty: 'Indeed, the most noble of you with Allah is the most pious of you.' And upon the one who is characterized by trustworthiness of private parts and wealth, and fulfillment of trust is connected to piety, so he must be the one looked to. But the Prophet of Allah mentioned that the leaders (al-a'imma) are from Quraysh, and the Companions of the Messenger of Allah sought from Quraysh and made a covenant for them, so the matter was turned over to them for reasons. Among them: First, that the imamate, along with the matter of religion, includes the matter of kingship and politics, so piety in that requires a lineage that is not ascetic nor deterred from its origin, so it was referred to the lineage that has always been zealous in them since they were known for majesty and ability, along with what is said: the Quran was revealed in the language of Quraysh. And from what is clarified: that it is necessary to consider two matters: as long as prophethood was known among one people and kingship among another, the matter of politics was in the hands of kings and religion in the hands of the prophets, upon them peace, as they said to a prophet of theirs: 'Appoint for us a king that we may fight in the way of Allah' — the verse. So likewise in this, the matter of religion was in everyone who undertook it, and the matter of kingship and politics was among a specific people whose majesty of status appeared among creation. He confirms: that lineage and descent call to noble deeds and interests and deter from ugly deeds and obscenities. So he who has deterrents and commands from aspects — as if he is in the common knowledge of the matter or in the covenant and foundations of the imamate. Then the scholars preferred Quraysh over other tribes and peoples in the matter of marriage, and upon that was the matter of combat among them: that they were not considered suitable matches for them, and upon that the Messenger of Allah treated them. Thus they consider others as having the right to the matter of the caliphate. And the specification may bear — and Allah knows best — two other aspects: First: that seeking that among all tribes and regions is a difficult matter, so it was eased by being referred to one tribe, and thus the greatest burdens were repelled from them. Then it is known of this tribe that it returns to a tract that makes it easy for people to examine all of its inhabitants and to succeed in finding who is suitable for the matter, whereas if it were referred to the whole, examination of all regions and tribes would be required, and for one who undertakes to reach the goal, it would cause waste of the matters for which imams are needed. Therefore He chose for them to seek among them. Second: He knows that there will always be found among them someone suitable for the affairs of Muslims, so He pointed to them with the knowledge that the sought one is found among them if they were fair and thorough in their search.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage provides a multi-layered argument for restricting the imamate to the Quraysh tribe. The author begins by acknowledging that piety and trustworthiness are the Quranic criteria for nobility, yet the Prophet's statement 'the imams are from Quraysh' and the Companions' practice show that the position is limited to Quraysh. The first reason given is that the imamate combines religious authority with political kingship, requiring a lineage that possesses both religious respect and worldly power without excessive asceticism. Quraysh historically had such nobility and capability, and the Quran's revelation in their language reinforces their suitability. The author draws an analogy from the Israelites who asked for a king while prophetic authority remained separate, arguing that in Islam the imam unites both roles. He then supports this with the principle that noble lineage encourages good deeds and deters evil, and that scholars recognized Quraysh's superiority in marriage compatibility, indicating their elevated status. After this, two additional justifications are offered. First, a practical reason: searching all tribes for a qualified candidate would be impractical and would waste time and resources, whereas restricting to a single well-known tribe (Quraysh) makes the selection easier. Second, a divine indication: Allah knows that Quraysh will always produce someone suitable for the task, so He directed the choice to them, provided that people act fairly and diligently in selecting from among them.

Key Terms
الإمامة
الملك
السياسة
قريش
التقوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The restriction to Quraysh is based on mere tribalism or racial superiority.
Correction: The author shows that the restriction is due to the imamate's unique combination of religious and political authority, requiring a lineage with both qualities, supported by practical and linguistic considerations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first reason the imamate was restricted to Quraysh?
A) Quraysh are the most pious tribe.
B) The imamate includes religion and kingship, requiring a lineage with both.
C) The Prophet ﷺ was from Quraysh, so they must rule.
D) Quraysh were the only tribe that accepted Islam.

Key Takeaway
The imamate's fusion of religious and political authority necessitates a leader from a tribe like Quraysh that historically embodies both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the leaders you support or admire, do you unconsciously separate their spiritual image from their practical competence? This lesson insists they must be one. Where in your life have you compartmentalized religion and politics, and what would change if you united them starting today?
?
Your family lineage and background shape your opportunities and responsibilities. Are you using your heritage as a source of strength to lead with integrity, or are you hiding behind humility to avoid the burden of influence? What is one specific step you can take this week to live up to the noble expectations your lineage (whether Quraysh or not) places on you?
?
If you were entrusted with a community's affairs tomorrow, what evidence would you give that you possess both piety and the ability to govern? This lesson challenges you to prepare for leadership by cultivating both your inner faith and your worldly skills—what is the one skill you will start developing today to become a more complete leader?

Two Further Justifications for the Quraysh Restriction
The author provides two supplementary justifications: practical ease and divine knowledge of perpetual suitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional reasons for restricting imamate to Quraysh: the practical ease of selection and Allah's foreknowledge of their perpetual suitability.

Core Idea
Restricting to Quraysh is not arbitrary but a merciful simplification and divine guidance.

After presenting the main argument based on the nature of the imamate, the author offers two further considerations that reinforce the restriction. The first is a practical justification: seeking a qualified imam from all tribes would be extremely difficult and cause neglect of important matters, so limiting the search to Quraysh eases the burden. The second is based on divine knowledge: Allah knows that Quraysh will always produce someone fit for the task, provided the search is fair and thorough.

Guided Path
1
Read the two aspects
Read the Arabic and translation for two additional justifications. Notice that they are introduced as 'two other aspects'.
These aspects complement the first reason, not replace it.
2
Understand the practical reason
The author argues that searching all tribes and regions is impractical; narrowing to one tribe makes the selection process manageable and prevents waste of time and resources.
This shows the wisdom behind the restriction as a form of relief (takhfif).
3
Understand the divine reason
Allah knows that Quraysh will always have someone suitable, so He directed the choice to them. The condition is that Muslims must be fair and diligent in choosing.
This is a matter of divine guidance, not mere human decision.

?

?
Allah's foreknowledge that a suitable person will always exist among Quraysh implies that He has already provided for the community's needs. What does this teach you about your own anxiety regarding the future of the Muslim community, and how should it change your level of trust in Allah's plan?

Qualities Required of an Imam
The author enumerates the qualities an imam must possess to fulfill the responsibilities of the caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the essential qualities a caliph (imam) must possess to fulfill the diverse responsibilities of leadership in Islam.

Core Idea
A true imam combines breadth of character, piety, wisdom, courage, and detachment from worldly gain.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi lays out the foundational qualities required for the caliphate. The author connects each responsibility of the imam to a specific set of moral and spiritual attributes. As you read, notice how the imam is presented as a unique figure who must balance worldly governance with divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Try to grasp the overall structure before analyzing details.
The passage uses the phrase 'ثم يتصل به' repeatedly to introduce each responsibility.
2
Identify the five responsibilities
Look for each 'then connected to it are' statement. List the five areas of responsibility the author mentions.
These are: interacting with people, financial/marital rights, implementing divine judgments, injustices/disputes, and relations with non-Muslims.
3
Note the two requirements for judgments
Pay special attention to the two qualities needed for implementing Allah's judgments: insight (wisdom) and courage (not fearing blame).
These are explicitly stated as 'أمرين'.
4
Reflect on the overall message
Ask yourself why the author emphasizes these personal qualities rather than political or military strength.
This highlights that the caliphate is primarily a religious and moral office.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الخلافة أمر يتصل به مصالح الدنيا والآخرة يبتلى صاحبها بالأخلاق المختلفة التي لا يصير لها ، ولا يقوم بحفظ حدود الله تعالى مع جميع أهلها إلا من اتسع صدره وظهرت صحبته أصناف البشر ، وعرف معاملة كل نوع على ما عليه حدود الله تعالى في أمثالهم، ثم يتصل به حقوق الله تعالى في الأموال والأبضاع لا يقوم بوفائها إلا من عظم ورعه وتم تقواه وكرم خلقه. ثم يتصل به أحكام الله تعالى لا يصلح لوفائها إلا بأمرين: أحدهما بالتبصر في فنون الحكمة والعلم بأحكام الدين. والثاني: أن لا يبالي بما يناله فى ذات الله تعالى، ولا يخاف لومة لائمة فيما يرجو فيه مرضاة الله تعالى. ثم يتصل به أمور المظالم ومنازعات تقع بين الخلق لا يقدم المرء بوفائها إلا من بالغ في النصح الله تعالى، وتم زهده في الدنيا وظهرت صيانته للعرض، ثم تصل به الأمور التي بين أهل دين الله وغيرهم مما كان في الأمر بذلك مخالفة
Then the foundation is that the caliphate is a matter connected to the interests of this world and the Hereafter; its holder is tested with various morals which he cannot attain, and none can preserve the limits of Allah Almighty with all its people except one whose breast is spacious, whose companionship with all kinds of people is apparent, and who knows how to treat each type according to what the limits of Allah Almighty are regarding their likes. Then connected to it are the rights of Allah Almighty regarding wealth and private parts; none can fulfill them except one whose piety is great, whose God-consciousness is complete, and whose character is noble. Then connected to it are the judgments of Allah Almighty; none is suitable to fulfill them except through two things: first, insight into the branches of wisdom and knowledge of the rulings of religion; second, that he not care about what befalls him in the cause of Allah Almighty, nor fear the blame of any blamer in what he hopes to achieve the pleasure of Allah Almighty. Then connected to it are matters of injustices and disputes that occur among people; no one can undertake their fulfillment except one who is extreme in sincere advice for Allah Almighty, whose renunciation of the world is complete, and whose protection of his honor is apparent. Then connected to it are the matters between the people of Allah's religion and others, which involve opposition in that command.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage outlines the essential qualities an imam must have to execute the duties of the caliphate, which encompasses both worldly and religious interests. The author lists four main areas of responsibility and the corresponding attributes. First, the imam must have a broad mind and the ability to interact with all types of people, understanding how to treat each group according to divine limits. Second, regarding financial and marital rights, the imam must possess profound piety, God-consciousness, and noble character to fulfill them properly. Third, for implementing divine judgments, two qualities are necessary: wisdom and knowledge of religious rulings, and courage—not being deterred by personal harm or criticism when seeking Allah's pleasure. Fourth, for handling injustices and disputes, the imam must be sincerely devoted to God, detached from worldly gains, and protective of his integrity. Finally, the imam must deal with matters between Muslims and non-Muslims, which may involve opposition to his commands, requiring steadfastness.

الأخلاق
حدود الله
الورع
التقوى
التبصر
العلم
الزهد
الصيانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Leadership is only about power and political skill, not personal piety or morality.
Correction: The passage shows that the caliphate requires deep moral and spiritual qualities; without them, the imam cannot fulfill the duties.

Checkpoint
What two things are required for an imam to implement Allah's judgments effectively?
A) Wealth and lineage
B) Wisdom and courage
C) Popularity and strength
D) Rhetoric and persuasion

Key Takeaway
The imam's role demands a combination of spiritual, intellectual, and moral excellences to manage both worldly and religious affairs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are in a position of authority, do you genuinely seek to treat each person according to divine limits, or do you favor those who please you? How does the requirement of a 'spacious breast' challenge your current interactions?
?
Are you willing to face criticism and blame for fulfilling Allah's rights, or do you shrink back to avoid conflict? This lesson demands fearlessness in the cause of Allah – is there a situation in your life where you have compromised due to fear of people's opinions?
?
Reflect on your own sincerity and renunciation of worldly gain. Are you quick to defend your honor when slighted, or do you prioritize sincere advice for Allah even at the cost of your reputation?

Sects and Disagreements

Two Explanations for Quraysh's Caliphate
Nasafi presents two possible reasons for restricting the caliphate to Quraysh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rationales behind specifying the caliphate to Quraysh as presented by Imam Nasafi.

Core Idea
The caliphate's restriction to Quraysh balances practicality and divine foresight.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi offers two complementary explanations for why the caliphate is limited to the tribe of Quraysh. The first is a practical consideration that eases the burden of selecting a leader, while the second points to divine knowledge that suitable candidates will always be found among them. These insights help us appreciate both human wisdom and divine omniscience in Islamic governance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's style come through.
Notice the phrase 'والله أعلم' indicating humility.
2
Identify the two explanations
Find the two 'wajhan' (aspects). The first begins with 'أحدهما' and the second with 'والثاني'. Note how each is introduced.
3
Reflect on the wisdom behind each
Consider how the first explanation is practical (ease of search) and the second is theological (divine knowledge). Ask yourself why both are needed.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حق أمر الخلافة، وقد يحتمل التخصيص - والله أعلم - وجهين آخرين: أحدهما: أن طلب ذلك في جميع القبائل والآفاق أمر عسير فخفف حيث رد إلى قبيلة واحدة ودفع عنهم به أعظم المؤن، ثم كان المعروف من أمر هذه القبيلة أنها ترجع إلى بقعة يسهل على الناس النظر في جميع من يسكنها والظفر بمن يصلح للأمر مما إذا رد إلى الجملة وجب نظر ذلك في جميع البقاع والقبائل، ولمن تكلف البلوغ إلى المقصود يوجب تضييع الأمور وما لأجله احتيج إلى الأئمة، فلذلك اختار لهم الطلب فيهم. والثاني: يعلمه قد علم أنه يوجد فيهم من يصلح لأمور المسلمين أبدًا فأشار إليهم بما علم أن المطلوب يوجد فيهم لو أنصفوا وأمعنوا الطلب.
The matter of the caliphate, and it may admit specification — and Allah knows best — in two other aspects: One of them: that seeking that among all tribes and regions is a difficult matter, so it was lightened by being limited to one tribe and removing the greatest burdens from them. And [because] the known status of this tribe is that they belong to a locality where it is easy for people to examine all those who dwell therein and obtain whoever is suitable for the affair. Whereas if it were referred to the whole, it would necessitate examining all regions and tribes, and for one who undertakes to reach the objective it would cause the neglect of affairs and [neglect of] that for which imams are needed. Therefore He chose for them the search among them. The second: He knows — He knows that there will always be found among them those suitable for the affairs of the Muslims, so He pointed to them by [revealing] what He knows that the sought one is found among them if they were fair and conducted a thorough search.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage presents two possible explanations (wajhan) for why the caliphate is specified to the Quraysh tribe. The first explanation is practical: requiring the caliph to be from all tribes and regions would be extremely difficult; limiting it to one tribe eases the burden, especially since the Quraysh resided in a single locality (Mecca) where people could easily inspect candidates. Searching universally would lead to neglect of essential affairs. The second explanation is based on divine knowledge: God knows that suitable persons will always exist among Quraysh, so He pointed to them, implying that the required person is found among them if the people are fair and diligent in their search. The passage thus highlights both practical wisdom and divine omniscience behind the specification.

Key Terms
الخلافة
قريش
التخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The specification of Quraysh for caliphate is arbitrary.
Correction: The passage provides two rational explanations: practical ease and divine knowledge that suitable persons will always be found among them.

Checkpoint
According to the first explanation, why was the caliphate restricted to Quraysh?
A) Because they were the most powerful tribe
B) To ease the search for a qualified candidate
C) Because they were the only tribe with scholars
D) To avoid conflicts among tribes

Key Takeaway
Nasafi justifies the caliphate's restriction to Quraysh through a practical need for ease and a divine guarantee of qualified leadership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult and complex decision, do you resist limitations that actually lighten your burden? Reflect on a specific area where you have been fighting against constraints that might be Allah's way of making it easier for you. What would it look like to trust that limitation as divine wisdom?
?
Nasafi says that if the search were universal, it would cause neglect of affairs. Are you overcomplicating your own responsibilities by trying to consider every option rather than trusting the path Allah has simplified? Where in your life today can you accept a 'specification' from Allah and focus on what matters?
?
The second explanation states that suitable leaders always exist among Quraysh if people are fair and thorough. In your own community, are you being fair and thorough in recognizing righteous leadership, or are you influenced by worldly status? How can you actively seek or support qualified leaders with sincerity and thoroughness?

The Caliph's Many Qualities and Deeper Analysis
Nasafi enumerates the many qualities required for the caliph and notes Abu Mansur's deeper analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the caliphate requires a comprehensive set of moral and leadership qualities, and how Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi provided a deeper rational explanation for the Quraysh specification beyond mere textual evidence.

Core Idea
The caliphate demands a complete moral and political leader; Abu Mansur uncovered the wisdom behind the Quraysh condition.

This passage from Tabṣirat al-Adilla lays out the immense responsibilities of the caliph and the many virtues needed to fulfill them. Imam al-Nasafi then highlights how most theologians accepted the Quraysh condition based on text alone, while Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi probed deeper to reveal its underlying rationale. This lesson will walk through these requirements and the methodological shift.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the translation to understand the content. Notice the recurring phrase 'ثم يتصل به' which structures the enumeration.
The passage is long but organized.
2
Identify the various responsibilities connected to the caliphate
The passage lists several areas: upholding the limits of Allah, handling wealth and private rights, implementing Islamic rulings, resolving disputes, dealing with non-Muslims, managing wealth, and leading jihad. List them as you read.
3
Understand the qualities needed for each area
For each responsibility, note the specific virtue required: expanded breast, piety, knowledge, fearlessness, sincerity, detachment, justice, chastity, self-discipline, political insight, etc. This shows the caliph must be a paragon of virtue.
4
Appreciate the role of lineage (Quraysh)
The passage explains why lineage is important: most of these virtues are acquired through upbringing and following one's people. So Quraysh were specified because they historically possessed these qualities. This is not arbitrary tribalism but based on practical need.
5
Contrast the two approaches: textual vs. rational
The passage ends by noting that most theologians just accepted the textual evidence, but Shaykh Abu Mansur went deeper to uncover the inner meaning. This illustrates the Maturidi approach of combining revelation with rational investigation.
This is the key methodological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الخلافة أمر يتصل به مصالح الدنيا والآخرة يبتلى صاحبها بالأخلاق المختلفة التي لا يصير لها ، ولا يقوم بحفظ حدود الله تعالى مع جميع أهلها إلا من اتسع صدره وظهرت صحبته أصناف البشر ، وعرف معاملة كل نوع على ما عليه حدود الله تعالى في أمثالهم، ثم يتصل به حقوق الله تعالى في الأموال والأبضاع لا يقوم بوفائها إلا من عظم ورعه وتم تقواه وكرم خلقه. ثم يتصل به أحكام الله تعالى لا يصلح لوفائها إلا بأمرين: أحدهما بالتبصر في فنون الحكمة والعلم بأحكام الدين. والثاني: أن لا يبالي بما يناله فى ذات الله تعالى، ولا يخاف لومة لائمة فيما يرجو فيه مرضاة الله تعالى. ثم يتصل به أمور المظالم ومنازعات تقع بين الخلق لا يقدم المرء بوفائها إلا من بالغ في النصح الله تعالى، وتم زهده في الدنيا وظهرت صيانته للعرض، ثم تصل به الأمور التي بين أهل دين الله وغيرهم مما كان في الأمر بذلك مخالفة الدين والموافقة وهما وجهان يدعون إلى الميل والإيثار وإلى الجور والظلم، ولذلك قال الله عز وجل: وَلَا يَجْرِمَنَّكُمْ شَنَتَانُ قَوْمٍ عَلَى أَلَّا تَعْدِلُوا اعْدِلُوا ) الآية. ثم في القريب والبعيد وذلك يدعو إلى ما ذكرت، فيحتاج في ذلك إلى عفيف صالح يعظم في عينه قدر نعم الله تعالى، ويجل في قلبه قدر حقه ليقوم بوفاء ذلك مع أمور في ذلك يحتاج المرء فيه أن يجمع مع العلم بأحكام الله تعالى والقيام بأمور دينه أنواع آداب النفس والمعاشرة، والصحبة والبصيرة في أمر الملك والسياسة ومكارم الأخلاق في حق المعاملة وغير ذلك، لذلك شرط مع وجود الموافقة لما ذكر في الجنس، فإن أكثر تلك الأمور إنما تكون بالاقتداء بقومه والأخذ عن المربين له والتقويم من الذين بهم نشوءه وبهم يعرف أسباب السؤدد فبين النبي عليه السلام القوم الذين في جملتهم وجود لكل نوع من ذلك ولا قوة إلا بالله. ويتصل به أيضا أمر جهاد الأعداء مع حسن الدعوة مع اتصال أنواع الأموال التي تقع في الأيدي تحت طلب أربابها ، والصرف إلى المستحقين لها مما يخاف تلف ذلك لولا رعايتها وحسن النظر، فهي على ما ظهر من احتواء الناس على الأموال وشدة الحرص على أخذها، والله أعلم. فجميع المتكلمين من أصحابنا رحمهم الله تعالى - بنوا الأمر على مجرد الشرع الوارد في تعيين قريش دون الكشف عن معنى ذلك، والشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله - وشكر سعيد وأحسن عن أهل الحق جزاءه - كشف كل هذا الكشف،
Then the principle is that the caliphate is a matter connected to the interests of this world and the next. Its holder is tested by various moral characters which he cannot master, and none can uphold the limits of Allah the Exalted among all its people except one whose breast is expanded, whose companionship with all kinds of people is evident, and who knows how to deal with each type according to the limits of Allah concerning their likes. Then connected to it are the rights of Allah concerning wealth and private parts (fahisha) – none can fulfill them except one whose piety is great, whose taqwa is complete, and whose character is generous. Then connected to it are the rulings of Allah – none is fit to fulfill them except through two things: first, insight into the branches of wisdom and knowledge of the rulings of religion; second, that he does not care what may befall him for the sake of Allah, and does not fear the blame of any blamer in what he hopes for the pleasure of Allah. Then connected to it are matters of grievances and disputes that occur among people – none can advance to fulfill them except one who is sincere in advising for Allah, whose detachment from the world is complete, and whose protection of honor is evident. Then connected to it are the affairs between the people of Allah's religion and others, which involve both opposition and agreement in religion – two aspects that invite inclination and favoritism or injustice and oppression. Therefore Allah said: 'Let not hatred of a people incite you not to act justly; be just' (5:8). Then concerning relatives and strangers – that invites what I mentioned. Therefore there is need for a chaste, righteous person who magnifies in his eyes the value of Allah's blessings, and exalts in his heart the magnitude of His right, to fulfill that. Along with that, the person needs to combine, together with knowledge of Allah's rulings and establishing the affairs of His religion, types of self-discipline and social conduct, companionship and insight into the matter of kingship and politics, and noble moral traits concerning dealing and others. Therefore it was conditioned, along with the agreement to what was mentioned, regarding lineage – for most of these affairs are only acquired by following one's people, learning from one's educators, and being corrected by those with whom he grew up and through whom he learns the causes of nobility. Thus the Prophet (peace be upon him) indicated the people in whose totality exists every type of that. And there is no power except with Allah. Also connected to it is the matter of jihad against enemies with good invitation, along with the various types of wealth that fall into hands subject to the claim of their owners, and their expenditure to those who deserve them – which may be feared to be lost without proper care and good management. This is as appears from people's greed for wealth and intense desire to take it. And Allah knows best. So all the mutakallimun among our companions – may Allah have mercy on them – built the matter on the mere textual evidence regarding the designation of Quraysh without uncovering the meaning of that. But Shaykh Abu Mansur – may Allah have mercy on him, and may He thank him and reward him well on behalf of the people of truth – uncovered all this unveiling.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the many qualities required for a caliph, showing that the restriction to Quraysh is not arbitrary but based on the need for specific virtues. It then praises Shaykh Abu Mansur (al-Maturidi) for providing a deeper analysis beyond the mere textual evidence.

The passage begins by stating that the caliphate concerns the interests of both worlds. The caliph is tested by various moral characteristics. To uphold Allah's limits, he must have an expansive breast (patience), good companionship with all kinds of people, and knowledge of how to deal with each type according to Allah's limits.

Next, it mentions the rights of Allah regarding wealth and private parts (probably referring to financial and marital laws). Only a person with great piety, complete taqwa, and generous character can fulfill them.

Then, the rulings of Allah require two things: insight into wisdom and religious knowledge, and fearlessness in the path of Allah, not caring for others' blame.

Then, matters of grievances and disputes require sincerity in advising for Allah, detachment from the world, and protection of honor.

Then, dealings with people of other religions involve potential conflict and require justice, as the Quranic verse (5:8) commands. Both near and far relations invite partiality or injustice.

Thus, a chaste and righteous person is needed who values Allah's blessings and right. Additionally, he must combine self-discipline, social skills, companionship, political insight, and noble morals. Therefore, lineage (being from Quraysh) was stipulated because most of these qualities are acquired through upbringing and following one's people. So the Prophet indicated Quraysh because in them all these qualities can be found.

Finally, the passage notes that the mutakallimun (theologians) of the Maturidi school relied on the plain textual evidence for Quraysh without delving into the wisdom. But Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi uncovered all this inner meaning (kashf). This shows the difference between strict textualism and rational investigation of underlying reasons.

Key Terms
الخلافة
قريش
الشيخ أبو منصور
المتكلمون
حدود الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The requirement for the caliph to be from Quraysh is based solely on tribal bias or arbitrary preference.
Correction: The passage shows that the Quraysh specification is grounded in the need for specific moral and leadership qualities that were historically found in Quraysh. It is not tribalism but a practical necessity for the caliph to possess virtues nurtured through upbringing in that community.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Shaykh Abu Mansur do that the other mutakallimun did not?
A) He rejected the specification of Quraysh
B) He uncovered the deeper meaning behind the textual evidence
C) He added more conditions to the caliphate
D) He wrote a new book on the caliphate

Key Takeaway
Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi exemplified the method of rational investigation (kashf) to uncover the wisdom behind the textual specification of Quraysh for the caliphate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The caliph must combine knowledge of Allah's rulings with self-discipline, social conduct, and political insight. Which of these areas do you most lack, and what specific step will you take this week to improve it for the sake of Allah?
?
Abu Mansur dared to go beyond the plain text and uncover the wisdom behind it. What is one teaching or practice you follow without understanding its deeper purpose? Will you commit today to studying it until you uncover its wisdom?
?
The caliph must not fear the blame of any blamer when seeking Allah's pleasure. Think of a situation where you stayed quiet or compromised because you feared what others would say. If that situation repeats, what will you say or do differently, and how will you prepare now?

Qualities Required for the Imam After the Condition of Quraysh
Enumeration of three types of imam qualities plus Abu Mansur's expanded list.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific qualities required for an imam beyond the condition of being from Quraysh, as enumerated by the mutakallimun of hadith and expanded by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.

Core Idea
Leadership in Islam requires both religious scholarship and practical statesmanship.

The condition that the imam be from Quraysh is well-known, but what other qualities must he possess? This passage presents two complementary classifications of the imam's required attributes. First, the theologians of hadith outline three essential categories: religious knowledge, moral integrity, and political-military acumen. Then, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi provides a more detailed list that expands on each of these dimensions, emphasizing the comprehensive excellence needed for the caliphate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins by stating it will discuss the second type (qualities after Quraysh). Then it presents the three-type classification, followed by Abu Mansur's expanded list.
Pay attention to the transition from the first classification to the second.
2
Identify the three types from the mutakallimun of hadith
The passage divides qualities into three types: knowledge (to the level of ijtihad), justice and piety (with the minimum that testimony is acceptable), and political-military competence (knowledge of governance and warfare).
Each type is explicitly numbered in the Arabic.
3
Compare with Abu Mansur's fuller list
Abu Mansur adds many qualities beyond the three, such as high aspiration, chastity, composure, courage, kindness in governing, etc. Notice how his list is more comprehensive and covers both moral and practical leadership.
The list ends with 'وغير' indicating further qualities not enumerated.
4
Reflect on the wisdom behind the Quraysh condition
Ask yourself why the scholars emphasize these qualities alongside lineage. The passage suggests that the Quraysh condition is not mere genealogy but a basis for finding a leader with these virtues.
This connects to the common misunderstanding that lineage alone suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أوصاف من يصلح للإمامة بعد النسب من قريش والنوع الثاني: هو الكلام في أوصافه التي بها يصير أهلاً للإمامة عند كونه قرشيًا، قال متكلمو أهل الحديث: إن تلك الأوصاف مقسمة إلى ثلاثة أنواع: أحدها: أن يكون في العلم بالحلال والحرام وجميع الأحكام بلغ مبلغ المجتهدين. والثاني: أن يكون عدلاً ورعًا أدنى درجاته أن يكون مقبول الشهادة. والثالث: أن يكون مهتديًا إلى وجوه السياسات والتدابير عارفاً بمراتب الناس، حافظاً لهم عليها، عالماً بأسباب الحرب وتدابيرها. فأما ما ذكره الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله في أثناء كلامه في حكمة تخصيص الشرع الأئمة أنهم من قريش، وقصر الأمر في ذلك عليهم أنه ينبغي أن يكون جامعاً بين علم الأحكام والحلال والحرام، ومعاشرة الناس ومعاملتهم، وعلو الهمة، وصرف النفس عن الخبائث والطمع وبسط اليد في الأموال، والعفة عن الفروج والأبضاع والعدالة والورع، وبين قوة الصريعة وشدة الشكيمة، والقدرة على إنصاف المظلوم من الظالم، ورباطة الجأش والشجاعة والإقدام، وحسن القيام بتدابير الحروب وجر العساكر، والرفق في الإيالة، والقيام بأسباب السياسة، وغير
Qualities of one who is suitable for imama after lineage from Quraysh. And the second type: the discussion of his qualities by which he becomes qualified for imama when he is a Qurashī. The mutakallimun of the people of hadith said: These qualities are divided into three types. One of them: that he be in knowledge of the halal and haram and all rulings, reaching the level of the mujtahidun. The second: that he be just and pious, the minimum degree of which is that his testimony be acceptable. The third: that he be guided to the ways of politics and management, knowing the ranks of people, preserving them for them, knowing the causes of war and its management. As for what Shaykh Abu Mansur mentioned in the midst of his speech regarding the wisdom of the Law's specification of the imams being from Quraysh, and restriction of the matter to them: that he should be one who combines knowledge of rulings and halal and haram, and interaction with people and dealing with them, and high aspiration, and turning the soul away from foul things and greed, and open-handedness in wealth, and chastity regarding private parts and sexual organs, and justice and piety, and along with that strength of resolve and severity of determination, and ability to give justice to the oppressed from the oppressor, and composure, courage and boldness, and good management of war strategies and leading armies, and kindness in governing, and undertaking the means of politics, and other [qualities].
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a classification and expansion of the qualities needed for an imam after the condition of Quraysh. First, the author presents the view of the 'mutakallimun of the people of hadith' (theologians among traditionalists) who divide the necessary qualities into three types: (1) knowledge of legal rulings to the level of ijtihad, (2) justice and piety to the extent that their testimony is accepted in court, and (3) political and military acumen, including knowledge of warfare and administration. Then, the author quotes Abu Mansur al-Maturidi who provides a more detailed list of qualities that the imam should combine, covering religious knowledge, social skills, moral virtues, bravery, and administrative capability. Abu Mansur's list includes both the intellectual and moral dimensions, as well as practical leadership skills. The passage ends abruptly with 'وغير' indicating there are further qualities not enumerated.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the requirement of Quraysh is merely genealogical without consideration of merit.
Correction: The passage shows that the imam must possess a comprehensive set of qualities including knowledge, justice, piety, and political-military skill, demonstrating that lineage is a vehicle for these virtues, not an end in itself.

Key Takeaway
The imam must combine religious scholarship, moral integrity, and political-military competence, as elaborated by the mutakallimun and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.

?

?
When you consider the combination of knowledge, justice, and political skill required for leadership, how does this challenge your idea of what makes a good leader in your community? Are you preparing yourself—even in a small way—to embody these qualities?

Three Qualities of the Imam Beyond Quraysh Lineage
Al-Nasafi enumerates the three types of qualities for imama after Quraysh lineage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of qualities required for the imam according to the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith: religious knowledge, moral integrity, and political acumen.

Core Idea
The imam must combine religious knowledge, moral integrity, and political wisdom.

Al-Nasafi continues his discussion of imama by presenting the views of the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith on the qualities that make someone fit for the position, beyond the prerequisite of Quraysh lineage. They divide these qualities into three categories: knowledge of Islamic rulings, justice and piety, and political-military acumen. This passage sets the stage for later debates on which of these are strictly required.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three categories
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith explicitly divide the required attributes into three numbered types.
The enumeration uses 'أحدها', 'والثاني', 'والثالث'.
2
Understand each quality
For each category, consider what specific requirement is mentioned. For knowledge: 'reaching the level of mujtahidun'. For justice: 'his testimony is accepted'. For political skill: 'guided to ways of politics and policies, knowing ranks, and warfare.'
The minimum for justice is set as 'adna darajatihi' (its lowest degree) being acceptable testimony.
3
Reflect on the significance
These three qualities cover religious authority, moral character, and practical governance. Ask yourself why each might be important for a leader.
Later in the text, the author will discuss whether all three are strictly required or merely recommended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنوع الثاني: هو الكلام في أوصافه التي بها يصير أهلاً للإمامه عند كونه قرشيًا، قال متكلمو أهل الحديث : إن تلك الأوصاف مقسمة إلى ثلاثة أنواع: أحدها: أن يكون في العلم بالحلال والحرام وجميع الأحكام بلغ مبلغ المجتهدين. والثاني: أن يكون عدلاً ورعًا أدنى درجاته أن يكون مقبول الشهادة. والثالث: أن يكون مهتديًا إلى وجوه السياسات والتدابير عارفاً بمراتب الناس، حافظاً لهم عليها، عالما بأسباب الحرب وتدابيرها.
And the second type: it is speech about his attributes by which he becomes qualified for imama when he is a Qurayshite. The mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith said: Those attributes are divided into three types: First: that in knowledge of halal and haram and all rulings he reaches the level of the mujtahidun. Second: that he be just and pious, its lowest degree being that his testimony is accepted. Third: that he be guided to the ways of politics and policies, knowing the ranks of people, preserving them accordingly, knowledgeable of the causes of war and its strategies.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage classifies the attributes required for the imam beyond Quraysh lineage. The mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith divide these into three categories: (1) reaching ijtihad in knowledge of rulings, (2) being just and pious such that his testimony is accepted, and (3) possessing political acumen, knowledge of people's ranks, and military strategy. The author presents this enumeration as a baseline from which he will later argue that not all are strict conditions.

متكلمو أهل الحديث
مجتهدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All three qualities are strictly required for every imam.
Correction: The author presents these as a classification by Ahl al-Hadith but later argues that some may be desired rather than strictly required.

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge, justice, and political skill
B) Wealth, lineage, and strength
C) Piety, courage, and generosity
D) Wisdom, eloquence, and beauty

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith divide the imam's required qualities into three: religious knowledge, moral integrity, and political-military expertise.

Al-Maturidi's Qualities: Desirable, Not Conditions
Al-Nasafi presents al-Maturidi's list and argues it is not a condition for imama.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi distinguishes between desirable qualities for an imam and strict conditions for the validity of imama.

Core Idea
Righteous qualities are aspirational, not contractual.

Al-Maturidi compiled an ambitious list of attributes an ideal imam should possess. But does lacking one invalidate his leadership? Al-Nasafi clarifies that while these traits are highly desirable, they are not conditions for the contract of imama. This lesson unpacks his evidence from Islamic legal practice and history.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the list of qualities and the subsequent argument.
2
Identify the list of qualities
Note all the qualities al-Maturidi mentions. They cover knowledge, morality, governance, and military skills.
The list is extensive and meant to be comprehensive.
3
Notice the key distinction
Al-Nasafi explicitly states these are 'desirable and sought' (مرغوب فيه مطلوب), not conditions. Understand the difference.
A condition (شرط) is required for validity; a desirable trait is preferred but not required.
4
Examine the qadi analogy
Al-Nasafi argues that just as an unjust qadi is not automatically removed, an imam lacking some qualities does not lose his position.
This analogy from fiqh principles supports the argument.
5
Reflect on historical evidence
Most caliphs after the Rashidun did not reach ijtihad, yet their imama was accepted. Consider how this reinforces the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما ذكره الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله - في أثناء كلامه في حكمة تخصيص الشرع الأئمة أنهم من قريش، وقصر الأمر في ذلك عليهم أنه ينبغي أن يكون جامعا بين علم الأحكام والحلال والحرام، ومعاشرة الناس ومعاملتهم، وعلو الهمة، وصرف النفس عن الخبائث والطمع وبسط اليد في الأموال، والعفة عن الفروج والأبضاع والعدالة والورع، وبين قوة الصريعة وشدة الشكيمة، والقدرة على إنصاف المظلوم من الظالم، ورباطة الجأش والشجاعة والإقدام، وحسن القيام بتدابير الحروب وجر العساكر، والرفق في الإيالة، والقيام بأسباب السياسة، وغير ذلك مما اجتمع في أثناء كلامه، فذلك كله مرغوب فيه مطلوب في الإمام. فأما كون هذه الأشياء بأجمعها شرطًا لانعقاد الإمامة له وصيرورته أهلاً لها، فذلك غير ثابت عنه، ولم أقف عليه عن أحد من أصحابنا والرواية عن أصحابنا ظاهرة أن القاضي إذا جار لا ينعزل، وإن استحق العزل بل بقي قاضيًا، فكذا هذا ينبغي أن يكون كذلك في الإمام الأعظم. فيكون المختار من استجمع ما ذكرنا من الخصال، فأما وجودها فيه ليس بشرط لانعقاد الخلافة، وأكثر الخلفاء بعد الخلفاء الراشدين ليكونوا بلغوا في العلم مبلغ الاجتهاد، ومع ذلك اعتقد الناس صحة إمامتهم وافتراض طاعاتهم.
As for what Sheikh Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) mentioned in the course of his speech regarding the wisdom of the Law's specification that the imams be from Quraysh, and restricting the matter to them, that he should combine knowledge of rulings, halal and haram, social interaction with people and dealing with them, high ambition, turning the soul away from vile things and greed, generosity with wealth, chastity from private parts and sexual organs, justice and piety, strength of affray and severity of resolution, ability to grant justice to the oppressed from the oppressor, fortitude, courage and boldness, good management of war strategies and leading armies, gentleness in governance, and conducting means of politics, and other things that gathered in the course of his speech—all of that is desirable and sought in the imam. As for these things collectively being a condition for the imama to be contracted for him and becoming qualified for it, that is not established from him, and I have not come across it from any of our companions. The transmitted report from our companions is manifest that if a qadi is unjust, he is not removed, even if he deserves removal—rather he remains a qadi. Thus this should be likewise regarding the supreme imam. So the chosen one is he who combines the qualities we mentioned. However, their existence in him is not a condition for the contract of the caliphate, and most of the caliphs after the rightly-guided caliphs did not reach the level of ijtihad in knowledge, yet the people believed in the validity of their imama and the obligation of obeying them.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage presents al-Maturidi's extensive list of desirable traits for the imam—spanning knowledge, morality, governance, and military prowess—but then argues that these are not conditions (shurut) for imama. The author supports this by analogy with the qadi: if a qadi becomes unjust, he is still not automatically removed; similarly, the imam's lack of some qualities does not invalidate his imama. He further notes that most caliphs after the Rashidun did not reach ijtihad, yet their imama was accepted. Thus, the ideal imam combines these traits, but their absence does not nullify the contract.

Key Terms
شرط
انعقاد الإمامة
خلفاء راشدين
قاضي
اجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Imam must be a mujtahid (independent jurist) as a condition for the validity of his imama.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that most caliphs after the Rashidun were not mujtahids, yet their imama was valid and obedience to them was obligatory. Ijtihad and other qualities are desirable but not conditions.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument al-Nasafi makes about al-Maturidi's list of imama qualities?
A) They are all strict conditions for imama
B) They are desirable but not conditions
C) Only the first three are conditions
D) They apply only to the Rashidun caliphs

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi's extensive list of qualities defines an ideal imam, but these are aspirations, not contractual conditions—imama remains valid even if some are missing.

?

How does this lesson challenge your standard of leadership? Will you now reconsider your willingness to accept and obey those in authority, even when they fall short?
?
Think of a specific leader you have struggled to accept due to their perceived flaws. Based on al-Nasafi's argument, which of your objections are about desirables and which are about actual conditions? What would it cost you to change your attitude and support them despite their imperfections?
?
The lesson shows that most caliphs after the first four were not mujtahids, yet people believed in the validity of their imama. Do you currently accept the religious or political leadership in your life? If not, what is preventing you? Is your refusal based on a valid condition (like clear injustice or unbelief) or on a desirable quality (like not being learned enough)? How will you act on your answer today?

The Imam's Qualifications: Ijtihad Not Required, Strength Required
Al-Nasafi uses analogy with qadi to argue that ijtihad is not required but political strength is.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's argument that ijtihad is not a requirement for the imam, but political strength and capability are essential.

Core Idea
The imam does not need ijtihad—he needs political strength.

Al-Nasafi uses a clever analogy with a judge (qadi) to clarify the qualifications of an imam. Just as a judge does not need to be a mujtahid because he can rely on others' knowledge, so too the imam does not need ijtihad. However, the imam must be a strong and capable leader to fulfill the purposes of his office. This passage draws a sharp line between theological knowledge and political authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Al-Nasafi begins with 'He verifies it'—this is a tahqiq, a verification of a point.
The passage is concise but packed.
2
Identify the analogy
Al-Nasafi compares the imam to a qadi. What attribute does he deny to both? What attribute does he affirm only for the imam?
Focus on the negation and affirmation.
3
List the conditions for imam
The passage lists several qualities the imam must have: being a politician, strong, capable of executing judgments, etc. Write them down.
These are all related to political and military capability.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Why does Al-Nasafi say these conditions are necessary? Connect them to the purpose of imama: executing justice and protecting the realm.
The last phrase says: 'if he were not so, that for which the imam was appointed would not be achieved.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه: أن كون القاضي مجتهدًا ليس بشرط لما أنه يمكنه أن يقضي بعلم غيره، فينبغي أن يكون الأمر في الإمام على هذا، فأما كونه سائسًا قويًا قادرًا على تنفيذ الأحكام وإنصاف المظلوم من الظالم وسد الثغور وحماية البيضة وحفظ حدود دار الإسلام وجر العساكر، فينبغي أن يكون شرطًا إذ لو لم يكن كذلك لم يحصل ما نصب الإمام لأجله.
He verifies it: That the qadi being a mujtahid is not a condition because he can judge by the knowledge of another, so the matter should be the same regarding the imam. As for him being a politician, strong, capable of executing judgments, granting justice to the oppressed from the oppressor, closing the breaches, protecting the territory, preserving the borders of the abode of Islam, and leading armies—this should be a condition, for if he were not so, that for which the imam was appointed would not be achieved.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage provides a verification (tahqiq) using analogy with the qadi. The qadi does not need to be a mujtahid because he can rely on another's knowledge. Similarly, the imam does not need ijtihad. However, the imam must be politcally skilled, strong, and capable of executing justice, defending borders, and leading armies. This is a condition because the very purpose of imama—establishing justice and protecting the realm—cannot be achieved otherwise.

Correction: Al-Nasafi separates the two: ijtihad is not required, but political strength is required.

The imam's essential qualification is political strength, not ijtihad.

Refuting the Rafidah Claim of Imam's Universal Knowledge
Al-Nasafi refutes the Rafidah claim via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's refutation of the Rafidah claim that the imam must have universal knowledge, including the absurd consequence of knowing how to play the lute.

Core Idea
The Rafidah claim leads to absurdities like requiring the imam to know lute playing.

In this passage, al-Nasafi dismisses the Rafidah doctrine that the imam must know everything. He considers it foolishness and illustrates its absurdity by noting that some Rafidah even required the imam to know how to play the lute. This reductio ad absurdum shows the invalidity of their claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the term 'hamaqah' (foolishness) which sets the tone of refutation.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
The author points out that some Rafidah demanded the imam know even lute playing (ḍarb al-ʿūd). This is a clearly ridiculous requirement that exposes the implausibility of their claim.
The passage cuts off after mentioning an anecdote, but the core argument is complete.
3
Reflect on the refutation method
Consider why this is an effective refutation: by taking the claim to its logical extreme, al-Nasafi shows it leads to an absurdity, thereby proving its falsehood.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا معنى لحماقة الروافض أن الإمام ينبغي أن يكون عالما بكل الأمور، حتى ألزم بعضهم أنه ينبغي أن يكون عالمًا بضرب العود فالتزم ذلك، وحكى عن
And there is no meaning to the foolishness of the Rafidah that the imam should be knowledgeable of all matters, to the point that some of them required that he should be knowledgeable of playing the lute, and they committed to that, and it is related from
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage dismisses the Rafidah (Shi'a) claim that the imam must have universal knowledge. The author calls it 'foolishness' (hamaqah) and illustrates its absurdity: some Rafidah absurdly required the imam to know even the trivial skill of playing the lute (darb al-'ud), and they accepted that implication. The text then begins an anecdote (hukiya 'an) that likely further ridicules the claim, though the page ends. The point is clear: the Rafidah position leads to ridiculous consequences, proving its invalidity.

Key Takeaway

Why the Imam Does Not Need to Know the Unseen
Author refutes the Rafidi claim that the imam must know everything including the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Rafidi claim that the imam must have comprehensive knowledge, including knowledge of the unseen, is false according to Sunni theology.

Core Idea
The imam does not need to know everything; imamate is a succession, not a prophecy.

The Rafidah (a Shi'a sect) claimed that the imam must know all matters, even the unseen. This lesson examines the Sunni refutation of this claim, showing how it leads to absurd consequences and contradicts the status of prophethood. We'll see the author's argument that such a requirement elevates the caliphate above prophethood, which is tantamount to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's strong language: 'foolishness', 'ignorance', 'pure disbelief'.
2
Identify the Rafidi claim and its absurd examples
The author mentions extreme examples like knowing how to play the oud and the imam's dog being infallible. What does this tell you about the author's view of the claim?
These examples show the claim leads to ridiculous requirements.
3
Understand the practical purpose of the imam's knowledge
The author states that the imam's knowledge is needed for executing the affairs of the subjects. Why is this distinction important?
It limits the imam's required knowledge to practical governance.
4
Analyze the proof from prophethood
The author uses the hadith 'I am more knowledgeable of your religious affairs, and you are more knowledgeable of your worldly affairs.' How does this refute the Rafidi claim?
The Prophet himself distinguished between religious and worldly knowledge.
5
Recognize the theological conclusion
The author concludes that requiring the imam to know worldly affairs or the unseen elevates the caliphate above prophethood, which is disbelief. Why is this a powerful argument?
It shows the claim contradicts the fundamental relationship between prophethood and imamate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول الروافض في شمول علم الإمام قول باطل] ولا معنى لحماقة الروافض أن الإمام ينبغي أن يكون عالما بكل الأمور، حتى ألزم بعضهم أنه ينبغي أن يكون عالمًا بضرب العود فالتزم ذلك، وحكى عن بعضهم أن الإمام ينبغي أن يعلم الغيب، وهذا كما حكي عن بعضهم ينبغي أن يكون كلب الإمام معصوما وهذا تجاهل، ومن بلغ في الغباء هذه الغاية يعرض عن مناظرته. ثم علم الإمام يحتاج إليه لتنفيذ أمور الرعية، فما وراء ذلك من العلوم لا معنى لاشتراطه. يحققه أن الإمامة خلافة النبوة والنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: "أنا أعلم بأمور دينكم وأنتم أعلم بأمور دنياكم" فمن شرط أن يكون الإمام عالمًا بأمور الدنيا أو يعلم الغيب فقد جعل درجة الخلافة أعلى من درجة النبوة، وهذا كفر محض.
The saying of the Rafidah about the comprehensiveness of the imam's knowledge is a false saying. And there is no meaning to the foolishness of the Rafidah that the imam should be knowledgeable of all matters, to the extent that some of them insisted that he should be knowledgeable in playing the oud, and he complied with that. And it was narrated from some of them that the imam should know the unseen. This is like what was narrated from some of them that the imam's dog should be infallible. This is ignorance. And whoever reaches this extreme in stupidity, one should turn away from debating him. Then the imam's knowledge is needed for executing the affairs of the subjects. As for knowledge beyond that, there is no meaning to stipulating it. He proves it: That the imamate is the succession of prophethood, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'I am more knowledgeable of your religious affairs, and you are more knowledgeable of your worldly affairs.' So whoever stipulates that the imam should be knowledgeable of worldly affairs or know the unseen has made the rank of the caliphate higher than the rank of prophethood, and this is pure disbelief.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage refutes the Rafidi claim that the imam must have comprehensive knowledge, including knowledge of the unseen. The author calls it foolish and mentions extreme examples like knowing how to play the oud or the infallibility of the imam's dog. He argues that the imam only needs knowledge to manage the affairs of the people. He proves this by the fact that the imamate is a succession of prophethood, and the Prophet himself distinguished between religious and worldly knowledge. To require the imam to know worldly matters or the unseen elevates the caliphate above prophethood, which is disbelief.

Key Terms
روافض
علم الإمام
غيب
خلافة
نبوة
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imam must have comprehensive knowledge, including knowledge of the unseen, because he is the successor of the Prophet.
Correction: The imam only needs knowledge to manage the affairs of the people; requiring knowledge of worldly matters or the unseen elevates imamate above prophethood, which is disbelief.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the Rafidi claim that the imam must know the unseen as disbelief?
A) Because it is impossible
B) Because it elevates the caliphate above prophethood
C) Because the imam is not a prophet
D) Because knowledge of the unseen is only for Allah

Key Takeaway
The imam need not know the unseen; requiring it contradicts the Prophetic model and amounts to disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Do you ever expect your religious leaders to have flawless knowledge of every matter, including trivial worldly affairs? How does this lesson expose the danger of such an expectation? Will you repent from this attitude and adjust your view of leadership according to the Prophetic model?

Imam Need Not Be the Best of His Time
Author states that the imam need not be the most excellent, and presents the Ash'ari view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that the imam does not need to be the most excellent of his time, and the contrasting Ash'ari view.

Core Idea
The imam's virtue over others is not a requirement for valid imamate.

This lesson examines whether the imam must be the most excellent person of his era. The Hanafi-Maturidi school, along with several other groups, holds that it is not a condition. The Ash'ari school disagrees, requiring the imam to be the best available. We will read the author's presentation of these views and the scholars who held them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the author first states his school's view, then lists supporters, then mentions the opposing view.
The passage is cut off at the end due to page boundaries.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the two contrasting positions: that imamate does not require the imam to be the most excellent, and that it does. Note which scholars are associated with each.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matter is important for Islamic political thought. Does requiring the best person create practical difficulties? The Maturidi position allows more flexibility.

Source Text

فأما كونه أفضل أهل زمانه ليس بشرط عندنا نص عليه الشيخ أبو منصور - رحمه الله - في كتاب المقالات، بل إذا كان فاضلاً صالحًا للإمامة وعقدت له الإمامة انعقدت، وإن كان الأفضل منه موجودًا، وإليه ذهبت المعتزلة والحسين بن الفضل البجلي من متكلمي أهل الحديث وأبو العباس القلانسي ومحمد بن إسحق بن خزيمة. وذهب أبو الحسن الأشعري بأن إمامة المفضول لا تنعقد مع وجود الفاضل، ويقول: إن من الأوصاف التي يصير بها المرء أهلاً للإمامة أن لا يكون أحد من
As for him being the most excellent of his time, that is not a condition according to us. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) stated this in the book Al-Maqalat. Rather, if he is virtuous and suitable for imamate, and the imamate is contracted for him, it is contracted, even if someone more excellent exists. This is the view of the Mu'tazila, al-Husayn ibn al-Fadl al-Bajali from the theologians of the people of hadith, Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, and Muhammad ibn Ishaq ibn Khuzayma. And Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari held that the imamate of an inferior does not contract in the presence of a superior, and he says: Among the qualities by which a person becomes worthy of imamate is that there be no one among...
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage addresses whether the imam must be the best of his time. The author states that according to the Hanafis (and others), it is not a condition; a virtuous person suffices even if a better person exists. He lists the Mu'tazila and several hadith scholars who hold this view. He then presents the opposing view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari that the imamate of a less excellent person does not contract when a more excellent one is present.
